<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1244954174">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1244954174" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" class="left imgcol" title="Aubrey"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1440305114p2/4119460.jpg" alt="Aubrey" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1244954174?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" class="user" title="Aubrey">Aubrey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16669329-great-expectations" title="Hardcover 9781435140707" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=3-star" class="actionLinkLite">3-star</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand" class="actionLinkLite">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads" class="actionLinkLite">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2015" class="actionLinkLite">r-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1244954174">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13525545747561310353">3.5/5<br /><br />I doubt so many would have children, or adopt even, if they really looked at their motivations in the long run. This isn't about a lack of belief in human love, or decency, or ethical parenting. This is about what inevitably happens when socioeconomics controls all, the metaphors of criminality and debt getting tied up with child rearing in such a way that being blamed from one's own birth becomes a matter of course. What with people still being executed for being poor, children dying on th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13525545747561310353">3.5/5<br /><br />I doubt so many would have children, or adopt even, if they really looked at their motivations in the long run. This isn't about a lack of belief in human love, or decency, or ethical parenting. This is about what inevitably happens when socioeconomics controls all, the metaphors of criminality and debt getting tied up with child rearing in such a way that being blamed from one's own birth becomes a matter of course. What with people still being executed for being poor, children dying on the streets for not fitting a certain mold, dissociation from an early age with barely a hope and portion of luck that reality will ever ground again, it's a crime to visualize oneself as anything other than a pile of deposits and withdrawals. It's a wonder Pip and Estella came out of it the way they did.<br /><br />So. Here we have a couple of down-on-their-luck clones in one of the most vicious skeins of vicarious living that the fictional world ever did see. What qualities this particular portrayal has lies more in the thematic content than the structuring, as Dickens is one of the heaviest abusers of coincidental intersections of a strangely cohesive cast since the time there weren't enough humans to shake a stick at. Lucky for him, the mirrors he plays with of wealth, to youth, to condemnation, to extortion, to wealth, to youth, have enough going for them in terms of substantial ideological commentary. Money is the root of all evil, as well as what puts food on the tables of children. Should the tables begrudge this food, it is inherently difficult to grow up attaching worth to anything other than that tied up with profundity of finances. The less self-esteem and other alternate methods of evaluation get in the way, the better.<br /><br />Those who are born poor are condemned to poverty. Those who are born women are condemned to violence. These two doctrines may be swerved a smidge through perfect morality and criminal undertakings, but otherwise the system is a thorough one barring the odd utter submission and rags to riches kidnapping. In Dickens' time, a measure of escape could be found in colonialism and ghosts. In our time, we must rely on his far less guaranteed social tools of trust, acceptance, and an uncoupling of fulfilled expectations from work and cash and portable property. Despite what many would like to believe, this is not an individual undertaking. It is not Pip's solo passing over wealth that saves him.<br /><br />As mentioned previously, there are many mirrors to this, each a matter of money, violence, gender, class, proneness to drink, gambling, false hopes, bildungsroman by proxy, all of which Pip may have succumbed to under any combination of different circumstances. Cliché it may be, but the small things draw him fundamentally away from brutality. Maybe not to self-awareness and a less boring personality, but socioeconomic survival does not oft demand that.<br /><br />The more popular the classic author, the more delicate their audience; as Dickens takes the cake, I will not delve into what punishments are wrought on those who refuse to become a Maternal Charging Dock. Fuck the anti-semitic and anti-romani trash, though. Would be defenders, don't even try.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13525545747561310353">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11756190864220343054"><em>Reseña pendiente.</em> <br /><br />No sé si es tanta la fascinación por la trama en sí (Pip es casi una Cenicienta a quien un hada anónima le promete un futuro de <em>grandes esperanzas</em>) como por cada personaje en particular. <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_89675933">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89675933" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1652898-karen" class="left imgcol" title="·Karen·"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" alt="·Karen·" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/89675933?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 18, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1652898-karen" class="user" title="·Karen·">·Karen·</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18251.Great_Expectations" title="Paperback 9780140620160" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=19th-century" class="actionLinkLite">19th-century</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=favourites" class="actionLinkLite">favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=brits" class="actionLinkLite">brits</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer89675933">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5368689844376230731">+++SPOILERS! - but this is Great Expectations - surely you'll have seen at least one of the 17 film versions, even if you weren't forced to read it at school? ++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++<br /><br /><br /><strong>How do I love thee? Let me count the ways</strong><br /><br />When I was a child, and read as a child, I read thee as a fairy tale. The forlorn orphan boy who knows in his heart that wicked dark forces have deposited him in the wrong house and doomed him to drudgery that he does not deserve; a wicked stepmother beats</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5368689844376230731">+++SPOILERS! - but this is Great Expectations - surely you'll have seen at least one of the 17 film versions, even if you weren't forced to read it at school? ++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++<br /><br /><br /><strong>How do I love thee? Let me count the ways</strong><br /><br />When I was a child, and read as a child, I read thee as a fairy tale. The forlorn orphan boy who knows in his heart that wicked dark forces have deposited him in the wrong house and doomed him to drudgery that he does not deserve; a wicked stepmother beats him, an ineffectual father cannot protect him. One day a weird fairy godmother in a secret, closed up castle waves a magic wand and transforms that changeling life - or is she the wicked fairy? The one whose magic proves to be a curse? What ordeals must he face before he can make the fairy princess his own? Can he, will he ever make her his own?<br /><br />When I was young and idealistic, I read thee as a tale of spiritual growth. I condemned Pip for his callow rejection of fine old Joe, I saw him arrive at self-knowledge and self-judgement through his own shame at the source of his riches, and work through the futility of his life as a leisured leech to final recognition that human dignity rests not on fine clothes and Latin phrases, but on pride in the fruits of your own labour and love and decency and loyalty and generosity. I was moved by his kindness to Miss Havisham and his fine compassionate treatment of Magwitch, his revelation at last, his insight:<br /><br /><em>For now, my repugnance to him had all melted away, and in the hunted wounded shackled creature who held my hand in his, I only saw a man who had meant to be my benefactor, and who had felt affectionately, gratefully and generously towards me with great constancy through a series of years. I only saw in him a much better man than I had been to Joe</em> (Chapter 54)<br /><br />When I became interested in politics I read thee as a paradigmatic tale of the nineteenth century. Pip as representative of a whole society, upwardly mobile, joining the ranks of the emerging middle class, acquiring all the badges of this new state: an education that fits him for nothing more than appearing to be a gentleman, the correct clothing to identify him as such, the boy-servant who has no purpose but to prove the status of his master, the membership of a club of dubious purpose and much rowdy 'fun' of the Hooray Henry stripe - <em>There was a gay fiction among us that we were constantly enjoying ourselves, and a skeleton truth that we never did</em>. And, of course, debts. Easy come, easy go. And where does the filthy lucre come from? How can this idle and frivolous lifestyle be funded? Why, from the profits of colonial trade of course, the financial basis of Britain's industrialisation and modernisation. Criminal, oh yes. No wonder he feels the guilt; he bears the guilt of a whole nation, its wealth dependent on exploiting its colonies and robbing the true workers of their just rewards. <br /><br />When I had had enough of stories of revenge and redemption and higher moral justice I read thee for the grotesque comedy of Mrs Pocket, the pompous absurdity of Pumblechook and Wopsle, for Wemmick's crenellated castle with drawbridge and Aged Parent and, most delightful of all, Trabb's boy. Trabb's jeering, taunting, mocking boy, always 'happening to be everywhere where he had no business' - and thank goodness he is, for he it is who leads Herbert and Startop to rescue Pip from Orlick at the limekiln.<br /><br /><em>For the present, under the circumstances, we deemed it prudent to make rather light of the matter to Trabb's boy; who I am convinced would have been much affected by disappointment, if he had known that his intervention saved me from the limekiln. Not that Trabb's boy was of a malignant nature, but that he had too much spare vivacity, and that it was in his constitution to want variety and excitement at anyone's expense. When we parted, I presented him with two guineas (which seemed to meet his views), and told him that I was sorry ever to have had an ill opinion of him (which made no impression on him at all).</em><br /><br />So droll. <br /><br />This time my eye was taken by Orlick. Orlick - Ole Nick? He shadows Pip at every turn, his evil other. In him coalesces all the violent aggression of unbridled ambition - Pip's foil or the manifestation of his guilt? The agent of his animosity? <br /><br />Keep reading, there's always more.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5368689844376230731">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_430745020">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/430745020" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/10665178-sara" class="left imgcol" title="Sara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451449917p2/10665178.jpg" alt="Sara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/430745020?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10665178-sara" class="user" title="Sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/518850.Great_Expectations" title="Hardcover 9780679405795" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10665178-sara?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10665178-sara?shelf=novels-and-shorts" class="actionLinkLite">novels-and-shorts</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10665178-sara?shelf=movies-i-have-seen" class="actionLinkLite">movies-i-have-seen</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10665178-sara?shelf=book-group-reads" class="actionLinkLite">book-group-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer430745020">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3686495106196709453">It's hard to review a classic. People have been reviewing Great Expectations for 150 years. I found out not all the original reviews were positive but sometimes time is in your favor when it comes to reviews.<br /><br />The only other Dickens novel I have read is A Christmas Carol. I have read that book a million times. I have started, but not finished, other books by Dickens. I was hesitant to start this and I wondered if I could finish it. For some reason, Dickens has always intimidated me and I'm not sur</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3686495106196709453">It's hard to review a classic. People have been reviewing Great Expectations for 150 years. I found out not all the original reviews were positive but sometimes time is in your favor when it comes to reviews.<br /><br />The only other Dickens novel I have read is A Christmas Carol. I have read that book a million times. I have started, but not finished, other books by Dickens. I was hesitant to start this and I wondered if I could finish it. For some reason, Dickens has always intimidated me and I'm not sure why.<br /><br />After the first page, I was hooked. I was careful not to read any of the introductions or even the fly-leaf. I have found when reading modern day intros to classic texts, spoilers abound. I've been disappointed that they give away the entire plot and the ending because they assume if you're reading this classic, you've read it before.<br /><br />Great Expectations is the story of Pip. He is an orphan who, due to a mysterious benefactor, comes into a goodly sum of money. This is written in first person and is told by an older Pip, a Pip who is not only older but wiser. It may be a rags-to-riches story, but it's not necessarily a happy story. Of course, there is some wonderful humor in this book and some wonderful characters. I love his descriptions of his people and how you can hear them talk, how you can see the shapes of their faces, the movements of their hands. In some ways this novel is bittersweet and very true to life in how people behave and how people think. The ending is different than the ending that was serialized. In my version, the original ending is added as an appendix. I like the novel ending much better. <br /><br />Beautiful prose! There were sentences I had to read several times and just let the words fall off of my tongue. I liked the use of repetition to emphasize certain points such as the "mud, mud, mud" of London on a rainy day.<br /><br />Here are some of the passages I enjoyed:<br /><br />"a contemptible, clumsy, sulky booby"<br /><br />"Mr. Wopsle's great-aunt conquered a confirmed habit of living into which she had fallen. . . " It took awhile for me to realize that she died.<br /><br />"That was a memorable day to me, for it made great changes in me. But it is the same with any life. Imagine one selected day struck out of it, and think how different its course would have been. Pause you who read this, and think for a moment of the long chain of iron or gold . . . that would never have bound you, but for the formation of the first link on one memorable day."<br /><br />I had no idea this phrase was so old (1860) - "Let HIM make a tool of me afresh and again?"<br /><br />I truly enjoyed this book and I am looking forward to reading more Dickens!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3686495106196709453">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_168850355">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168850355" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa" class="left imgcol" title="Vanessa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1366468327p2/4636758.jpg" alt="Vanessa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/168850355?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 31, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa" class="user" title="Vanessa">Vanessa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/2619.Great_Expectations" title="Paperback 9780141439563" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4636758-vanessa?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4636758-vanessa?shelf=2015-tbr" class="actionLinkLite">2015-tbr</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer168850355">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15750161028708198912">Another year, another Dickens, and this time I FINALLY got to <em>Great Expectations</em>. This is undoubtedly one of Dickens most well-known and best-loved works, and I'm happy that I finally bit the bullet and picked it up again after years away from it. I have to say that it's not one of my personal favourites though.<br /><br />For those who aren't familiar with the general plot, <em>Great Expectations</em> follows Pip throughout his life. As a young boy he lives with his overbearing sister and her kindly husband Joe, an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15750161028708198912">Another year, another Dickens, and this time I FINALLY got to <em>Great Expectations</em>. This is undoubtedly one of Dickens most well-known and best-loved works, and I'm happy that I finally bit the bullet and picked it up again after years away from it. I have to say that it's not one of my personal favourites though.<br /><br />For those who aren't familiar with the general plot, <em>Great Expectations</em> follows Pip throughout his life. As a young boy he lives with his overbearing sister and her kindly husband Joe, and looks to be apprenticed by Joe as a blacksmith. However, one day he is summoned to meet Miss Havisham, a strange woman who lives alone shut up in her grand house with her cold but beautiful ward Estella. This opens opportunities (both well-known and secret) that allow Pip to make a vastly different life for himself.<br /><br />That's the best I can do without giving anything specifically away. Overall I enjoyed this book - the cast of characters for the most part had very diverse personalities, and with a variety of memorable names they simply lifted off the page. However, this book is a slow burner, and although I do enjoy slow books that are character-driven for the most part, I found at times that Pip in particular was quite a hard character to be stuck in the head of. He was very selfish for the majority of the book, and it could be quite uncomfortable to read at times.<br /><br />I also thought that for the majority of the book not a great deal happened. The book is split into three parts: the first encompassing Pip's childhood; the second encompassing his years growing further in London to meet his 'expectations'; the third detailing how he deals with a certain event that takes place at the end of the second part, and how it affects his life from there on out. I enjoyed the sections from his childhood, and everything with Joe and Miss Havisham was excellent - they are both such fantastic, well-drawn characters, and in particular Joe is an incredibly sympathetic and sweet characters that I just adored. However, much of part 2 I found to be particularly dull, and I can't say my attention was all that invested in it.<br /><br />The majority of part 3 and the ending however definitely picked up, and there were some quite intense scenes that took me completely by surprise. Although I knew the main twist in the book already, it didn't particularly bother me, and there was enough in the book that I wasn't previously aware of to make up for it. My edition also interestingly contained the original ending that Dickens had wrote for the novel - although I liked the ending he went with, dare I say that I enjoyed the original ending more? It had a certain darkness to it that felt all the more realistic and honest.<br /><br />Overall, this was an enjoyable read, but I wouldn't say it's one of Dickens' best. If you aren't sure where to start with Dickens, I would say that this is probably a good place to start with as most people are somewhat familiar with the story. Personally however, I think <em>Oliver Twist</em> is a much more entertaining starting-off point.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15750161028708198912">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_35954275">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35954275" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days" class="left imgcol" title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420332941p2/395599.jpg" alt="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/35954275?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days" class="user" title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)">Shannon (Giraffe Days)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/2705546-great-expectations" title="Paperback 9780199219766" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=2008" class="actionLinkLite">2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer35954275">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer149375645635134272">Even if you haven't read this or seen any of the many movie or tv adaptations, you would know something of the story. This is the one about Pip, an escaped convict, a beautiful but cruel girl called Estella, and the corpse-like Miss Havisham. It's about a little boy called Pip who was raised by his much older sister, Mrs Joe, and her husband, Joe, the village blacksmith. Joe is a role model and father figure as well as Pip's best friend, while Mrs Joe is sharp-tongued and aggressive - between he</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText149375645635134272">Even if you haven't read this or seen any of the many movie or tv adaptations, you would know something of the story. This is the one about Pip, an escaped convict, a beautiful but cruel girl called Estella, and the corpse-like Miss Havisham. It's about a little boy called Pip who was raised by his much older sister, Mrs Joe, and her husband, Joe, the village blacksmith. Joe is a role model and father figure as well as Pip's best friend, while Mrs Joe is sharp-tongued and aggressive - between her and Joe's uncle, Mr Pumblechook, Pip is made to feel worthless and one step away from becoming the most ungrateful wretch on the face of the Earth.<br /><br />A chance encounter as a boy of about 8 with a convict who has escaped from a Hulk (an old un-seaworthy ship used as a prison while Britain dithered over where to send the convicts) sees Pip do an act of kindness - mostly out of fear - and keep his first secret from Joe. <br /><br />The reclusive Miss Havisham - who was jilted at the altar at a quarter to nine and lives in suspended time, still wearing her decaying wedding dress, with one shoe on, the clocks all stopped and her mansion falling down around her - sends for Pip to be a playmate for her adopted daughter Estella, Pip is made to feel ashamed of his rough hands and coarse boots, his ignorance and weakness, by Estella's cruel remarks. Despite her blatant cruelty, Pip falls in love with her and torments himself with wanting her. When, after about four years as an apprentice blacksmith, Pip learns from a London lawyer, Mr Jaggers, that he has a patron who will pay his way into society but who insists on keeping their identity secret, Pip is sure it must be Miss Havisham and that she plans to raise him up so he can have Estella. <br /><br /><br />The truth about the matter is probably quite predictable - I guessed it, though I couldn't figure out the logistics of it - but I don't want to say anything just in case. What I can talk about instead is Dickens' prose, language, diction, style, whatever you want to call it, and the reluctance of people like me to read him. (This is the first Dickens book I've read.) It is true that authors at the time were paid by the word, and it is quite true that Dickens is pretty wordy. Don't let that put you off though. He's no more wordy than a lot of other authors - George Eliot, for example, or Tolstoy or Thackeray. But every word is carefully chosen, and while a few times the diction was alien to me because the language is now disused, you can still get the meaning from the context. The story is in the details, though, so try to avoid the temptation of skimming long passages: a great deal is told and revealed in very few words and you need to read every sentence to fully understand and appreciate the story. Impressions are gained through clever structure, repetition and other stylistic devices - it's a "show", not "tell", kind of book.<br /><br />It's interesting, while reading volume 1, to note that you have an older, wiser Pip narrating the story of a much younger, more naive Pip - at times our perception is coloured by the older Pip's derisiveness; you get the sense that he's shaking his head in bewilderment or rueful amusement. It's quite clever, what he reveals and what he keeps mum about in order for the story to maintain a level of suspense and excitement. You know he's stringing you along but he does such a good job of it, you can't hold it against him. One of the things this book really succeeds at is the positing of a younger Pip and an older Pip, presented at the same time, the same character and yet different, and showing how Pip changes over the years and why, until the two finally merge. It takes talent to manipulate the dual Pips that way, and keep a handle on their emotional maturation.<br /><br />Miss Havisham is one of those larger-than-life characters who becomes famous in their own right: the bizarre, gothic spinster, embittered by her personal tragedy, warping the mind of Estella to make her into a woman who will hurt men the way Miss H was hurt. The frozen-in-time, decaying mansion - the room with the rotting wedding cake on the table, everything covered in cobwebs and bugs, with mice crawling in the walls. It's incredibly vivid and grotesque, and it's these highly exaggerated scenes and characters that make Dickens such a memorable writer.<br /><br />I suppose the moral of the story centres around Pip and how, when he comes into money and a better position in society, he becomes snobbish and unlikeable, looking down on Joe and his childhood friend, Biddy, and becoming class conscious in the worst way. He puts social position and class, etiquette and learning, gentility and leisure, above his friends - such misplaced value, and an inability to appreciate what's really worth appreciating, are fairly common themes but are dealt with with surprising subtlety here - I think because Pip is narrating, and he doesn't really come out and say "I was selfish and unthinking and thought myself better than the man who raised me, but I repent." He doesn't need to - it's quite clear. And, naturally, he learns from his mistakes.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="149375645635134272">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/7668522-carol" class="left imgcol" title="Carol"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451627423p2/7668522.jpg" alt="Carol" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/382736659?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7668522-carol" class="user" title="Carol">Carol</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/8113168-great-expectations" title="Paperback 9780143106272" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=read-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-2014</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=buddy-read-jean" class="actionLinkLite">buddy-read-jean</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer382736659">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2687028077807652189">I'm a somewhat <em>green</em> Dickens fan (having only read <a title="A Christmas Carol by Charles Dickens" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5326.A_Christmas_Carol">A Christmas Carol</a>), but loved this 150th Anniversary Edition of Great Expectations! As in A Christmas Carol, this tale often portrays an eerie atmosphere with creepy characters.....like the mysterious escaped convict Abel Magwitch who threatens Pip's young life in the graveyard, the jealous and <em>revengeful</em> Old Orlick with his evil ways, and the embittered and <em>decaying</em> Mrs. Havisham who pines for lost love and leads Pip astray.<p>In this coming-of-ag</p></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2687028077807652189">I'm a somewhat <em>green</em> Dickens fan (having only read <a title="A Christmas Carol by Charles Dickens" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5326.A_Christmas_Carol">A Christmas Carol</a>), but loved this 150th Anniversary Edition of Great Expectations! As in A Christmas Carol, this tale often portrays an eerie atmosphere with creepy characters.....like the mysterious escaped convict Abel Magwitch who threatens Pip's young life in the graveyard, the jealous and <em>revengeful</em> Old Orlick with his evil ways, and the embittered and <em>decaying</em> Mrs. Havisham who pines for lost love and leads Pip astray.<p>In this coming-of-age story, the orphaned Pip is brought up by the <em>heavy</em> hand of his ill-tempered older sister and (thankfully) her caring, but poor blacksmith husband Joe (my favorite character), and as Pip becomes a young man, his secret dream to leave his present sphere of life (as Joe's apprentice) and be brought up as a gentleman (winning the hand of the beautiful Estella) seems impossible until a mysterious benefactor makes his dream come true.....or so he thinks.</p><p>Written with meticulous character detail, this suspenseful, funny and entertaining adventure follows Pip's "educational" and eventful life ultimately leading to the <em>true</em> source of his "Great Expectations".</p></span>

  <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="left imgcol" title="Sue"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" alt="Sue" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1240781062?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="user" title="Sue">Sue</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/2619.Great_Expectations" title="Paperback 9780141439563" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=britain" class="actionLinkLite">britain</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=my-own-books-2015" class="actionLinkLite">my-own-books-2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1240781062">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1658834487619372181">A revelation and a delight---those were my reactions on reading, then on finishing, Great Expectations, first read, and not enjoyed while in high school, only slightly remembered from that time(vague recall about who his actual patron might be). <br /><br />This second experience, oh so many years later, has reawakened the joy of reading the Victorian serial novel. I looked forward to picking this book up each time I did so. I chuckled and laughed with some of Dickens words, names and descriptions, enjoyed</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1658834487619372181">A revelation and a delight---those were my reactions on reading, then on finishing, Great Expectations, first read, and not enjoyed while in high school, only slightly remembered from that time(vague recall about who his actual patron might be). <br /><br />This second experience, oh so many years later, has reawakened the joy of reading the Victorian serial novel. I looked forward to picking this book up each time I did so. I chuckled and laughed with some of Dickens words, names and descriptions, enjoyed the characters he developed, and the variety of emotions he could elicit. What a master.<br /><br />Among my favorites---Pip's progress toward self knowledge itself, Wemmick and the Aged One, Herbert and so many in that little village, who are all drawn so well. And Dickens' descriptive skills--of the marshes, the boats on the Thames, Newgate, the death masks. So many details that complement and forward the action.<br /><br />Now I want and plan to read more of Dickens as soon as I'm able. As always it's the scheduling that is the hardest part.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1658834487619372181">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rM55Oxiod/ewjKW91Q8nE3qTgr8amEj3HLbVeREgLqrJGkbo6npjDTxU0QarbsGhM2RBplNFD112v9Dr3bXY7w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9tXqOx+xzBba0EA50C5bUSu7qtkhoxlPGjozJkeOFN6TAdXo7WPY7FYINIKuT73jYkxpwGh+XuVwMza0ixvimw==')})
  }

It is the beginning of an infatuation that will last a lifetime. He befriends her husband, an influential businessman, and as their paths cross and re-cross over the years, Mme Arnoux remains the constant, unattainable love of Moreau’s life. Blending love story, historical authenticity, and satire, Sentimental Education is one of the great French novels of the nineteenth century.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a The fate of this beloved country home symbolizes the future of England itself in E. M. Forster\'s exploration of social, economic, and philosophical trends, as exemplified by three families: the Schlegels, symbolizing the idealistic and intellectual aspect of the upper classes; the Wilcoxes, representing upper-class pragmatism and materialism; and the Basts, embodying the aspirations of the lower classes. Conventionally regarded as a history play, much of it is in fact conspicuously invented fiction, and Kastan argues that the non-historical, comic plot does not simply parody the historical action but by its existence raises questions about the very nature of history. The full and engaging introduction devotes extensive discussion to the play\'s language, indicating how its insistent economic vocabulary provides texture for the social concerns of the play and focuses attention on the central relationship between value and political authority. Kastan also covers the recurrence of the word &amp;quot;honor&amp;quot; in the text and the role that women play.  Appendices provide the sources of 1 Henry IV, discussions of Shakespeare\'s metrics, and the history of the manuscript.  The appendix on casting features a doubling chart to show which characters may be played by one actor.  Photographic images of the original Q0 Fragment, which is assumed to have been printed in Peter Short\'s printing house in 1598, appear in the fifth appendix. Finally, a reference section provides a list of abbreviations and references, a catalog of Shakespeare’s works and works partly by Shakespeare, and citations for the modern productions mentioned in the text, other collated editions of Shakespeare\'s work, and other related reading.\n     \n    The Arden Shakespeare has developed a reputation as the pre-eminent critical edition of Shakespeare for its exceptional scholarship, reflected in the thoroughness of each volume. An introduction comprehensively contextualizes the play, chronicling the history and culture that surrounded and influenced Shakespeare at the time of its writing and performance, and closely surveying critical approaches to the work. Detailed appendices address problems like dating and casting, and analyze the differing Quarto and Folio sources. A full commentary by one or more of the play’s foremost contemporary scholars illuminates the text, glossing unfamiliar terms and drawing from an abundance of research and expertise to explain allusions and significant background information. Highly informative and accessible, Arden offers the fullest experience of Shakespeare available to a reader.\n  \n&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"8635676185630936871\" href=\"#\" Silas hoards a treasure that kills his spirit until fate steals it from him and replaces it with a golden-haired founding child. Where she came from, who her parents were, and who really stole the gold are the secrets that permeate this moving tale of guilt and innocence. When conditions in the mining community deteriorate even further, Lantier finds himself leading a strike that could mean starvation or salvation for all.\n\n•New translation\n• Includes introduction,     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">About Charles Dickens</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens"><img alt="239579" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1387078070p2/239579.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer2729848925877019203"> A prolific 19th Century author of short stories, plays, novellas, novels, fiction and non-fiction; during his lifetime Dickens became known the world over for his remarkable characters, his mastery of prose in the telling of their lives, and his depictions of the social classes, morals and values of his times. Some considered him the spokesman for the poor, for he definitely brought much awarenes</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2729848925877019203"> A prolific 19th Century author of short stories, plays, novellas, novels, fiction and non-fiction; during his lifetime Dickens became known the world over for his remarkable characters, his mastery of prose in the telling of their lives, and his depictions of the social classes, morals and values of his times. Some considered him the spokesman for the poor, for he definitely brought much awareness to their plight, the downtrodden and the have-nots. He had his share of critics, like Virginia Woolf and Henry James, but also many admirers, even into the 21st Century.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2729848925877019203">...more</a>

    </div>
